قاضی کے لئے ہدیہ قبول کرنے کا حکم ولا يقبل هدية إلا من ذي رحم محرم أو ممن جرت عادته قبل القضاء بمهاداته لأن الأول صلة الرحم والثاني ليس للقضاء بل جرى على العادة و فيما وراء ذلك يصير آكلاً بقضائه حتى لو كانت للقريب خصومة لا يقبل هديته وكذا إذا زاد المهدي على المعتاد أو كانت له خصومة لأنه لأجل القضاء فيتحاماه ولا يحضر دعوة إلا أن تكون عامة لأن الخاصة لأجل القضاء فيتهم بالإجابة بخلاف العامة ويدخل في هذا الجواب قريبه وهو قولهما وعن محمد أنه يجيبه وإن كانت خاصةً كالهدية والخاصة ما لو علم المضيف أن القاضي لا يجيبه لما جهزها ترجمہ اور قاضی کسی کا ہدیہ قبول نہ کرے سوائے اپنے ذی رحم محرم کے یا ایسے شخص کے جس کے ساتھ قاضی ہونے سے پہلے باہمی ہدیہ لینے دینے کی عادت جاری تھی۔ کیونکہ اول صلہ رحمی کے طور پر ہے۔ اور دوسرا قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ عادت کے طور پر ہے اور اس کے علاوہ میں قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے کھانے والا ہوگا۔ حتی کہ اگر کسی قریبی رشتہ دار کا مقدمہ ہو تو اس کا ہدیہ بھی قبول نہ کرے۔ اور اسی طرح عادت کے طور پر ہدیہ دینے والے نے جب معتاد سے زیادہ بھیجا یا اس کا کوئی مقدمہ ہو ۔ کیونکہ یہ قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہے۔ لہذا اس سے پرہیز کرے اور کسی دعوت میں نہ جائے الا یہ کہ دعوت عام ہو۔ کیونکہ خصوصی دعوت قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہوگی ۔ پس اس کو قبول کرنے میں متہم ہو گا۔ برخلاف دعوت عامہ کے اور اس حکم میں قاضی کا قریبی بھی شامل ہے اور یہی شیخین کا قول ہے اور امام محمد سے مروی ہے کہ ذی رحم محرم کی دعوت قبول کرے اگر چہ دعوت خاصہ ہو جیسے ہدیہ قبول کرنا ہے۔ اور دعوت خاصہ یہ ہے کہ اگر میزبان کو یہ معلوم ہو جائے کہ قاضی نہیں آئے گا تو وہ دعوت تیار نہ کرے۔تشریح ہدیہ اور رشوت میں فرق یہ ہے کہ رشوت اس شرط کے ساتھ دی جاتی ہے کہ لینے والا اس کی مدد کرے اور ہدیہ میں یہ شرط نہیں ہوتی ۔
Ruling on the judge accepting gifts: He shall not accept a gift except from a mahram relative or from someone with whom he had the custom of exchanging gifts before his judgeship, because the first is for maintaining family ties, and the second is not because of his judgeship but rather customary. Beyond that, he becomes one who eats due to his judgeship. So much so that if the relative has a lawsuit, he should not accept his gift. Likewise, if the giver increases beyond the customary amount or if he has a lawsuit, because it is due to his judgeship, so he should avoid it. And he should not attend a feast unless it is a general invitation, because a specific invitation is due to his judgeship, so he would be suspected of responding favorably, unlike a general invitation. This ruling includes his relative. This is the view of the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And it is narrated from Muhammad that he may accept the invitation even if it is specific, like a gift. The specific invitation is that if the host knows that the judge will not come, he would not prepare the food. Explanation: The difference between a gift and a bribe is that a bribe is given with the condition that the recipient will help him, whereas a gift does not have this condition.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage details strict rules for a judge regarding gifts and invitations to maintain impartiality. The basic rule: a judge may only accept gifts from a mahram relative (because it is family obligation) or from a person with whom he had a pre-existing gift exchange custom (because it is not due to his position). If the relative becomes a litigant, the gift is forbidden. If the custom-giver increases the amount or becomes a litigant, also forbidden. Regarding invitations, the judge may only attend general public feasts (e.g., wedding walima open to all). A specific invitation is suspected to be for influence. Imam Muhammad allows accepting from a mahram even if the invitation is specific. The passage concludes by distinguishing gift (hadiyya) from bribe (rishwa): a bribe has an explicit condition of help, while a gift does not, but a gift to a judge is still prohibited because it may influence judgement.

Key Terms
ذی رحم محرم (dhi rahm mahram): A blood relative who is permanently forbidden to marry; e.g., father, brother, uncle.
رشوت (rishwah): Bribe: something given with an explicit or implicit condition to influence the judge's decision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A judge receives a gift from his paternal aunt (father's sister) who has never had a case. The judge had no prior gift exchange with her. According to the Hanafi school, should he accept?
A) Yes, because she is a mahram relative.
B) Yes, because no case is pending.
C) No, because the gift is beyond the usual custom (she never gave before, so no custom established).
D) No, because an aunt is not considered a mahram.

Key Takeaway
A judge's acceptance of gifts or invitations is strictly limited to avoid any appearance of bias; only gifts from mahram relatives or through pre-established custom are permissible, and even those are forbidden if the giver has a pending case.

When a Judge Must Refrain from Judgment and the Company of Jurists in Court
Explains when a judge must refrain from judgment and the recommended company of fuqaha'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a judge must refrain from judgment and explain the basis for extending the prohibition beyond anger.

Core Idea
A judge must not give judgment when his heart is occupied — anger is a specific example, not the limit.

This passage from the Book of Etiquette of the Judge (Kitab al-Adab al-Qadi) presents two key rules: the prohibition of judging while distracted and the recommendation to have jurists present for consultation. The prohibition is based on a well-known hadith which the Hanafi school interprets broadly by identifying the effective cause (ʿilla) as preoccupation of the heart. The second rule, regarding company, aims to prevent suspicion and enhance the quality of judgments through consultation with fuqaha'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its prohibition
First, recognize that the act under discussion is the judge giving judgment (qada). The passage states that the judge should not give judgment in certain states: distress, need to urinate/defecate, or any state that occupies the heart.
The prohibition is at the level of makruh tahrimi — the judgment is valid but sinful.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'La yaqdi al-qadi wa huwa ghadban' (The judge should not judge while he is angry). This is a single-transmission (ahad) hadith, making its thubut (certainty of transmission) zanni.
The hadith is found in Sunan Abi Dawud, Ibn Majah, and others.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (manner of proof)
The prohibition (la yaf'al) indicates impermissibility. The explicit mention of anger is a specific example, but the ʿilla (effective cause) is the preoccupation of the heart. The Hanafi school extends the ruling to any state that distracts the judge, such as hunger, thirst, or emotional distress.
This extension is based on the principle that the ʿilla determines the scope of the ruling.
4
Note the company recommendation
The passage also recommends that those who used to sit with the judge before his appointment should continue to do so, to avoid suspicion of corruption. It is also mustahab (recommended) that a group of fuqaha' be present for consultation.
This is based on the practice of the early caliphs (Uthman and Abu Bakr).

كتاب الادب القاضي
رف الہدایہ جلد نہم الت میں یا رنج کی حالت میں یا پیشاب پائخانہ کے تقاضہ کی حالت میں فیصلہ نہ دے۔ حاصل یہ کہ قاضی ایسی حالت میں فیصلہ نہ ے جب اس کا قلب دوسری طرف مشغول ہو اور اسکی دليل حديث لا يقضى القاضى وهو غضبان ہے۔ یہ حدیث چونکہ حلول ہے اس لئے نغضبان سے خاص طور پر غصہ کی حالت مراد نہ ہوگی۔ بلکہ ہر وہ حالت مراد ہوگی جو قاضی کے قلب کو مشغول کر ے اور ذہنی یکسوئی کو فوت کر دے۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جولوگ قاضی ہونے سے پہلے اس کے پاس بیٹھتے تھے وہ اب بھی اس کے ساتھ بیٹھیں۔ کیونکہ تنہا بیٹھنے ہی رشوت لینے یا ظلم کے ساتھ متہم ہونے کا اندیشہ ہے۔ چنانچہ مروی ہے کہ حضرت عثمان جب تک چار صحابہ موجود نہ ہوتے کچھ حکم نہ یتے۔ اور مستحب یہ ہے کہ قاضی کی مجلس میں فقہاء کی ایک جماعت بیٹھے ۔ اور قاضی ان سے مشورہ کرتا رہے۔ چنانچہ صدیق اکبر کی مجلس یں حضرت عمر ، عثمان علی رضی اللہ عنہم حاضر رہتے تھے۔
Book of Etiquette of the Judge. The commentary of Hidayah volume nine: He should not give judgment in a state of distress, or in a state of need to urinate or defecate. The conclusion is that the judge should not give judgment in any state when his heart is occupied with something else. Its evidence is the hadith: 'The judge should not judge while he is angry.' Because this hadith is general, so 'angry' does not specifically mean only the state of anger, but rather every state that occupies the judge's heart and removes mental concentration. The author of Hidayah says: Those who used to sit with him before he became a judge should still sit with him, because sitting alone gives the suspicion of taking bribes or committing injustice. Thus it is narrated that Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) would not give any ruling until four Companions were present. And it is recommended that a group of jurists sit in the judge's court, and the judge should consult them. Thus in the court of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, Umar and Uthman (may Allah be pleased with them) were present.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Etiquette of the Judge (Kitab al-Adab al-Qadi) establishes the ruling that a judge must refrain from giving judgment when his mental focus is compromised. The primary evidence is the well-known hadith 'La yaqdi al-qadi wa huwa ghadban' (The judge should not judge while he is angry). The Hanafi school, as explained in Al-Hidayah, interprets this hadith broadly: the effective cause ('illa) is not anger specifically but any state that distracts the heart and mind. Thus, the prohibition extends to states of extreme hunger, thirst, need to relieve oneself, or emotional distress. The text then discusses the company of the judge: those who associated with him before his appointment should continue to do so to prevent suspicion of corruption. The practice of Caliph Uthman and the recommendation of having fuqaha' present for consultation are cited. This is a ruling with proof, combining a textual evidence (hadith) and rational reasoning (istihsan) to prevent suspicion and ensure justice.

Key Terms
غضبان (ghadban): Angry state; used in the hadith as a specific example of a state that preoccupies the heart.
قلب (qalb): Heart/mind; the seat of concentration and judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis for extending the prohibition of judging while angry to other states?
A) The hadith explicitly lists all prohibited states.
B) The ʿilla (effective cause) is preoccupation of the heart, which is common to anger and similar states.
C) The judge's opinion is always invalid if he is not calm.
D) The hadith is weak, so scholars use qiyas to cover all states.

Key Takeaway
A judge must avoid giving judgment in any state that compromises mental focus, and it is recommended to have jurists present for consultation.

Ruling on Accepting Gifts and Invitations by a Judge
Ruling on gifts and invitations for a judge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two exceptions under which a Hanafi judge may accept a gift, explain the distinction between private and general invitations, and apply the ruling to scenarios involving relatives and prior custom.

Core Idea
A judge may only accept gifts from a close relative or prior custom; all other gifts are prohibited as bribes.

This lesson examines the strict rules governing a judge's acceptance of gifts and invitations in Hanafi fiqh. Based on a hadith of the Prophet, the principle is that any gift to a judge is presumed to be due to his position unless a clear family tie or prior custom removes that presumption. The same logic applies to invitations: general invitations are allowed, but private ones are prohibited. We will also explore an internal disagreement between the two shaykhs and Imam Muhammad regarding relatives' invitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its evidence
State the general prohibition on judges accepting gifts and cite the hadith of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi as proof.
The hadith: 'Why did he not sit in his father's house...' shows that gifts to officials are suspect.
2
List the two exceptions for gifts and their rationale
Name the two exceptions: (1) dhi rahim mahram (close relative), because the gift is likely for family ties; (2) one with prior custom of gift exchange, because it is not due to the judgeship.
These exceptions are only valid if no lawsuit is pending and the gift does not exceed customary amount.
3
Recognize additional restrictions that nullify exceptions
Even from allowed sources, the gift is prohibited if the giver has a lawsuit or if the amount exceeds the customary level.
The presence of a lawsuit indicates the gift is likely to influence the judge.
4
Understand the parallel rule for invitations
A judge may attend only general invitations (where the public is invited). Private invitations are prohibited because they are presumed to be due to the judgeship.
The definition of a private invitation: the host would not prepare the meal if the judge were not coming.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on private invitations from relatives
Imam Muhammad permits accepting a private invitation from a dhi rahim mahram by analogy with gifts. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) prohibit it. The relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

-1 ذی رحم محرم یعنی قرابتدار
اشرف البدایہ جلد شما
۲۔ جس کے ساتھ قاضی ہونے سے پہلے ہدیہ لینے دینے کی عادت جاری تھی ۔ اور قاضی کے لئے ہدیہ قبول کرنے کے عدم جواز پر یہ حدیث حجت ہے۔ بخاری شریف میں ابو حمید الساعدی سے مروی ہے ان رسول الله استعمل رجلاً من الازد على الصدقة فلما قدم قال هذا لكم وهذا اهدى لى فقال رسول الله فهلا جلس في بيت ابيه او بيت امه فينظر ا يهدى له ام لا یعنی رسول اکرم صلى الله عليه وسلم نے قبیلہ ازد کے ایک شخص کو صدقہ پر مقرر کیا۔ پھر جب وہ آیا تو کہا کہ یہ تمہارا مال ہے اور یہ مجھے ہدیہ دیا گیا ہے۔ پس رسول اکرم صلى الله عليه وسلم نے فرمایا وہ کیوں اپنی ماں یا اپنے باپ کے گھر نہیں بیٹھا کی دیکھتا کہ اسکو ہدیہ دیا جاتا ہے یا نہیں ۔ حضرت عمر نے حضرت ابو ہریرہ کو عامل مقرر کیا پھر حضرت ابو ہریر اپنے ساتھ مال لائے۔ پس حضرت عمر نے پوچھا کہ یہ تو کہاں سے لایا ؟ ابو ہریرہ نے کہا کہ مجھے متواتر ہدیہ ملے ہیں ۔ حضرت عمر نے فرمایا کہ اللہ کے دشمن تو کیوں نہیں اپنے گھر بیٹھا کہ دیکھتا کہ تجھے ہدیہ دیا جاتا ہے یا نہیں ۔ پھر حضرت ابو ہریرہ سے یہ مال لیکر بیت المال میں جمع کر دیا۔ اور ذی رتم محرم کا ہدیہ قبول کرنا اس لئے جائز ہے کہ یہ ہدیہ صلہ رحمی کی وجہ سے ہے۔ قاضی ہونے کو وجہ سے نہیں ہے۔ اور اسی طرح قاضی ہونے سے پہلے جس کے ساتھ ہدیہ کے لین دین کی عادت جاری تھی ، قاضی ہونے کے بعد اس کا ہدیہ قبول کرنا قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے نہیں ہو گا۔ بلکہ سابقہ عادت کے تحت ہوگا۔ اور جو ہدیہ، قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے نہ ہو اس کے قبول کرنے میں کوئی مضائقہ نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے ذی رحم محرم کا ہدیہ اور جس کے ساتھ پہلے سے عادت جاری تھی اس کا ہدیہ قبول کرنا جائز ہے۔ اور ان دونوں کے علاوہ کا ہد یہ چونکہ قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہے اس لئے ان دونوں کے علاوہ سے ہدیہ قبول کرنا جائز نہ ہوگا۔ حتی کہ اگر کسی قرابتدار کا مقدمہ زیر سماعت ہو تو قاضی اس کا ہد یہ بھی قبول نہ کرے یا جس کے ساتھ پہلے سے عادت جاری تھی اگر اس کا مقدمہ زیر سماعت ہو تو اس کا ہدیہ بھی قبول نہ کرے یا اس نے مقدار معتاد سے زیادہ بھیجا تو زیادہ مقدار کو قبول نہ کرے اگر چہ اس کا مقدمہ بھی زیر سماعت نہ ہو ۔ کیونکہ یہ قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہے۔ لہذا اس سے پر ہیز کرے۔
رہی یہ بات کہ جن صورتوں میں قاضی کے لئے ہدیہ قبول کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اگر ان صورتوں میں قاضی نے بعد یہ قبول کر لیا تو اس کا کیا کرے۔ بعض مشائخ نے فرمایا کہ اس کو بیت المال میں داخل کر دے اور عامۃ العلماء کا مذہب یہ ہے کہ جن لوگوں سے لیا تھا اگر ان کو پہچانتا ہو تو ان کو واپس کر دے اور اگر ان کو نہ پہچانتا ہو یا پہچانتا ہومگر دوری کی وجہ سے واپس کرنا مجعد رہو تو اس کو بیت المال میں جمع کر دے اور اس کا حکم لقطہ کا حکم ہو گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ قاضی خصوصی دعوت قبول نہ کرے البتہ عمومی دعوت قبول کر سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ خصوصی دعوت قاضی ہونے کی وجہ سے ہوگی ۔ اور اس کو قبول کرنے میں متہم ہوگا۔ برخلاف عمومی دعوت کے کیونکہ عمومی دعوت قبول کر سکتا ہے ۔ کیونکہ عمومی دعوت قبول کرنے میں قاضی متہم نہ ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ اس حکم میں یعنی خصوصی دعوت قبول نہ کرنے میں قاضی کا
Ruling on accepting gifts for the judge. The text: 'And he should not accept a gift except from a dhi rahim mahram (close relative) or from one whose custom of exchanging gifts existed before his judgeship, because the first is for maintaining family ties, and the second is not for the judgeship but rather according to custom. Beyond that, he becomes a consumer due to his judgeship. So even if the relative has a lawsuit, he should not accept his gift. Similarly, if the giver increases beyond the customary amount or if he has a lawsuit, he should not accept because it is for the sake of the judgeship; thus he should avoid it. And he should not attend any invitation unless it is general, because a private invitation is for the sake of the judgeship, so he is suspected of responding, unlike a general one. And this ruling includes his relative, and this is the statement of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). And from Muhammad (ibn al-Hasan) that he should respond even if it is private, like the gift. And a private invitation is one where the host knows that if the judge does not come, he would not prepare it.' Translation and commentary: The judge should not accept a gift from anyone except his dhi rahim mahram or someone with whom he had a custom of exchanging gifts before his judgeship. Because the first is for maintaining family ties, and the second is not due to judgeship but according to custom. In other cases, he becomes a consumer (of bribe) due to his judgeship. Even if a close relative has a lawsuit, he should not accept his gift. Similarly, if the customary giver increases beyond the usual or if he has a lawsuit, he should not accept because it is for the judgeship. So he should avoid it. And he should not attend any invitation unless it is general, because a private invitation is due to judgeship; thus he will be suspected of responding, unlike a general invitation. And this ruling includes the judge's relative. This is the statement of the two (Sheikhain). And from Imam Muhammad it is narrated that he should accept the invitation of a dhi rahim mahram even if it is private, just as he accepts a gift. And a private invitation is when the host knows that if the judge does not come, he would not prepare the meal. Clarification: The difference between a gift and a bribe is that a bribe is given with the condition that the recipient will help him, whereas a gift does not have this condition. The author of Quduri says: The judge should not accept a gift from anyone except two persons: 1) a dhi rahim mahram, i.e., a relative; 2) one with whom the custom of giving and taking gifts existed before his judgeship. And the evidence for the impermissibility of a judge accepting gifts is this hadith: In Sahih al-Bukhari, it is narrated from Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi that the Messenger of Allah appointed a man from the tribe of Azd over the charity. When he returned, he said, 'This is for you, and this was gifted to me.' The Messenger of Allah said, 'Why did he not sit in his father's house or his mother's house and see whether he is given gifts or not?' That is, the Prophet appointed a man from Azd over charity. When he came, he said, 'This is your property, and this was gifted to me.' So the Prophet said, 'Why did he not sit in his mother's or father's house and see whether he is given gifts or not?' Umar appointed Abu Hurairah as a collector, and he brought wealth with him. Umar asked, 'Where did you get this?' Abu Hurairah said, 'I received consecutive gifts.' Umar said, 'Enemy of Allah, why did you not sit in your house and see whether you are given gifts or not?' Then he took the wealth from Abu Hurairah and deposited it in the treasury. Accepting a gift from a dhi rahim mahram is permissible because this gift is due to family ties, not due to the judgeship. Similarly, accepting a gift from someone with whom there was a custom of exchange before the judgeship is permissible because it is due to prior custom, not due to judgeship. Any gift that is not due to the judgeship may be accepted without issue. So accepting a gift from a dhi rahim mahram and from one with prior custom is permissible. Gifts from others are due to judgeship, so it is not permissible to accept them. Even if a relative has a pending lawsuit, the judge should not accept his gift; or if the prior custom giver has a lawsuit, his gift is not accepted; or if he sends more than the customary amount, the extra should not be accepted even if the giver has no lawsuit, because it is due to judgeship. So he should avoid it. As for cases where it is not permissible for the judge to accept a gift, if he does accept it, what should he do? Some scholars said he should put it in the public treasury. The majority of scholars hold that if he knows the giver, he should return it; if he does not know him or knows him but returning is difficult due to distance, he should put it in the treasury, and its ruling is that of luqatah (lost property). The author of Hidayah says: The judge should not accept a private invitation, but he may accept a general invitation, because a private invitation is due to his judgeship, and he would be suspected for accepting it, unlike a general invitation. The author of Hidayah says: In this ruling, i.e., not accepting a private invitation, the judge's... [text cut off]
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage comprehensively covers the Hanafi ruling on gifts (hadiyah) and invitations (da'wah) for a judge. The basic ruling is that a judge is prohibited from accepting any gift from anyone because it is presumed to be due to his position. Two exceptions are made: (1) a gift from a dhi rahim mahram (a close relative whom he cannot marry), as it is likely due to family ties; (2) a gift from someone with whom the judge had a custom of exchanging gifts before his appointment, as it is not due to the judgeship. However, even these exceptions are nullified if the giver has a pending lawsuit or if the gift exceeds the customary amount. The same principle applies to invitations: a judge may only attend general invitations (where the public is invited), because a private invitation is presumed to be due to his position and thus prohibited. The text also records an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Muhammad permits accepting a private invitation from a dhi rahim mahram by analogy with the gift, while the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) maintain the general prohibition. The relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs. Additionally, the passage clarifies the distinction between a gift and a bribe: a bribe is conditioned on a favor, while a gift is unconditional, but both are generally prohibited for a judge. If the judge inadvertently accepts a prohibited gift, he must either return it to the giver if known, or deposit it in the public treasury (bayt al-mal) with the ruling of luqatah. The hadith of Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi (as narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari) is the primary evidence, where the Prophet criticized an appointed collector for accepting gifts. This is a ruling with proof, incorporating a clear textual evidence (hadith) and rational reasoning (istihsan) to prevent corruption.

Key Terms
هدية (hadiyah): Gift given to the judge; if given because of his position, it is prohibited as a form of bribe.
ذو رحم محرم (dhu rahim mahram): A relative within the prohibited degrees of marriage, i.e., a close blood relative whom one cannot marry.
دعوة خاصة (da'wah khassah): A specific invitation where the host would not prepare the meal if the judge were not coming.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, from which two categories of people may a judge accept a gift?
A) Any Muslim who has no lawsuit with him.
B) Only other judges or scholars.
C) A dhi rahim mahram (close relative) and someone with whom he had a custom of exchanging gifts before his judgeship.
D) Only the ruler or his appointees.

Key Takeaway
A judge's acceptance of gifts and private invitations is strictly regulated to prevent corruption; only gifts from close relatives or prior custom, and only general invitations, are allowed.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Ruling on Judge's Acceptance of Gifts
States the exceptions for judges accepting gifts with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two exceptions when a Hanafi judge may accept a gift and explain the hadith evidence proving the general prohibition.

Core Idea
A judge's gift is presumed due to his position; only gifts from mahram relatives or prior custom are exempt.

This passage from Quduri's matn establishes a strict rule: a judge may not accept gifts from anyone except two categories. The Prophet's hadith and Caliph Umar's practice show that gifts to officials are tainted by suspicion. We will examine the rationale, the exceptions, and the proper course if a prohibited gift is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
The text begins with the rule: a judge should not accept a gift from anyone except two persons. Determine the hukm level (makruh tahrimi).
The prohibition is strict; even gifts not intended as bribes are disallowed.
2
List the two exceptions
The first exception is a mahram relative (dhi rahm mahram). The second is a person with whom the judge had a habit of exchanging gifts before becoming a judge.
These exceptions are based on intent: the gift is not due to judgeship.
3
Locate the hadith dalil
The proof is from Sahih Bukhari: the Prophet rebuked a collector who accepted a gift, implying it was due to his position. The wording 'Why did he not sit in his father's house...' shows the gift was given because of his appointment.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for this ruling.
4
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The Prophet's rhetorical question indicates that if he were not in that position, he would not have received the gift. Thus, any gift to a judge is presumed to be due to his office unless proven otherwise.
This presumption is the basis for the prohibition.
5
Determine consequences of wrongful acceptance
If a judge accepts a prohibited gift, he must return it to the donor if known; if not, he deposits it in the treasury (bayt al-mal) with the ruling of a lost item (luqata).
The text provides two opinions; the majority view is return if possible.

لقطہ کا حکم ہو گا۔
Book of Etiquette of the Judge: The author of Quduri says that a judge should not accept a gift from anyone except two persons: 1. A close relative (dhi rahm mahram), i.e., a kinsman. 2. One with whom he had a habit of giving and receiving gifts before becoming a judge. And the proof for the impermissibility of a judge accepting a gift is this hadith: In Sahih Bukhari, from Abu Humayd al-Sa'idi: The Messenger of Allah appointed a man from Azd over the sadaqah. When he came, he said, 'This is for you, and this was gifted to me.' The Messenger of Allah said, 'Why did he not sit in his father's house or his mother's house and see whether he is given gifts or not?' That is, the Messenger of Allah appointed a man from the tribe of Azd over sadaqah. Then when he came, he said, 'This is your wealth, and this was given to me as a gift.' So the Messenger of Allah said, 'Why did he not sit in his mother's or father's house and see whether he is given gifts or not?' Umar appointed Abu Hurayra as an agent. Then Abu Hurayra brought wealth with him. Umar asked, 'Where did you bring this from?' Abu Hurayra said, 'I received continuous gifts.' Umar said, 'O enemy of Allah, why did you not sit in your house and see whether you are given gifts or not?' Then he took that wealth from Abu Hurayra and collected it in the treasury. And accepting a gift from a close relative is permissible because this gift is due to maintaining kinship ties, not due to being a judge. Similarly, for one with whom the habit of exchanging gifts was established before becoming a judge, after becoming a judge, accepting his gift is not due to being a judge, but due to the previous custom. And any gift that is not due to being a judge, there is no harm in accepting it. Therefore, accepting a gift from a close relative and from one with whom the custom was previously established is permissible. And a gift from other than these two is due to being a judge, so accepting it is not permissible. Even if a relative's case is sub judice, the judge should not accept his gift; or if a person with prior custom has a sub judice case, he should not accept his gift; or if the person sent more than the customary amount, he should not accept the excess even if his case is not sub judice, because it is due to being a judge. So he should avoid it. As for the question: In those cases where it is not permissible for a judge to accept a gift, if the judge has accepted it, what should he do? Some mashayikh said he should put it in the treasury (bayt al-mal). And the madhhab of the majority of scholars is that if he knows the people from whom he took, he should return it to them; if he does not know them, or knows them but returning is difficult due to distance, then he should collect it in the treasury, and its ruling is the ruling of a found object (luqata).
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidaya, commenting on Quduri's matn, presents the ruling that a judge should not accept gifts except from two categories: close relatives (dhi rahm mahram) and those with whom he had a prior habit of gift exchange. The proof is a hadith from Sahih Bukhari where the Prophet rebuked a collector who accepted gifts, implying that such gifts are due to the official position. The commentary also cites the practice of Umar with Abu Hurayra. The rationale is that gifts from these two exceptions are motivated by personal ties, not the judgeship. However, if a litigation is pending, even these exceptions become prohibited because the gift could influence the case. If a prohibited gift is accepted, the judge should return it if possible; otherwise, he should deposit it in the public treasury, treating it like a lost item.

Key Terms
ذی رحم محرم (dhi rahm mahram): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited, i.e., a mahram relative.
ہدیہ (hadiya): A gift given voluntarily, distinguished from bribery (rishwa) which is given to influence a decision.
لقطہ (luqata): A lost article found by someone; here applied to gifts that cannot be returned, to be treated as found property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, from which two categories may a judge accept a gift?
A) From any Muslim who does not have a case pending
B) From close relatives (dhi rahm mahram) and those with prior gift-giving custom
C) From litigants only if the gift is small
D) From anyone as long as the judge is not biased

Key Takeaway
A judge may only accept gifts from mahram relatives or from those with a prior gift-giving custom, and if a prohibited gift is accepted, it must be returned or deposited in the treasury.
